    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="7154c13843c64cc988cb11f2074b6c68">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '09e809bd-aa90-47e4-8f65-4f7617ff2056'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

                var session_id = 'A6E29B4383048E0A3385F595050F0A15';

                s.src = url;
                var x = document.getElementsByTagName('script')[0];
                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }

                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d29071636" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>Spherical Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>2.96</sub>Mg<sub>0.02</sub>Cu<sub>0.02</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (NFMCPP) cathode materials have been successfully synthesized via the combination of spray-drying and solid-state reaction method. The Mg/Cu co-doping can effectively mitigate the lattice volume change and promote the electronic conductivity of NFMCPP. While, the small NFMCPP particles with spherical geometry exhibit an enhanced structural stability, due to the high sphericity structure enables a low high-stress concentration and alleviated diffusion-induced stress during the extensive (de)sodiation cycling.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2b60051c-1b5d-4131-9084-374df9299656/anie202423296-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/2b60051c-1b5d-4131-9084-374df9299656/anie202423296-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4405357c-906b-4c14-a375-0b2b25759237/anie202423296-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2b60051c-1b5d-4131-9084-374df9299656/anie202423296-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d29071640" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (NFPP) has been regarded as the promising cathode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). However, the practical applications of NFPP are hindered by its high-volume changes, poor intrinsic electron conductivity and sluggish Na+ ions diffusion kinetics. Herein, a spray-drying and solid-state reaction method have been utilized to fabricate the spherical trace amount Mg/Cu co-doped Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (NFMCPP). The Mg/Cu co-doping can effectively mitigate the lattice volume change and promote the electronic conductivity of NFMCPP by reducing band gap between the conduction and valence bands. While, the unique spherical structured NFMCPP with a carbon film even coated on its surface ensures rapid electron transport. Moreover, small NFMCPP particles with spherical geometry demonstrate an alleviated diffusion-induced stress and enhanced structural stability, due to the high sphericity structure enables fluent Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion, leads to a low high-stress concentration and uniform stress/strain distribution during extensive (de)sodiation process. Consequently, the optimized spherical NFMCPP cathode materials exhibit an excellent rate capability and cyclic stability.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202423296-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202423296-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium ion battery (SIB) has been considered as a promising candidate for large-scale energy storage because of the rich abundance of sodium resources. To further improve the practical applications and commercialization of SIBs, it is vital to develop a cathode material with long cycle life and high safety.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423296-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423296-bib-0001_R_d29071628e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1">1</a></sup></span> To date, a wide range of compounds have been studied as SIB cathode materials, e.g. layered transition metal oxides,<span><sup><a href="#anie202423296-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423296-bib-0004_R_d29071628e533" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2">2</a></sup></span> polyanion compounds,<span><sup><a href="#anie202423296-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423296-bib-0010_R_d29071628e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3">3</a></sup></span> and Prussian blue analogues.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423296-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423296-bib-0013_R_d29071628e539" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4">4</a></sup></span> Among these developed cathode materials, polyanionic compounds have been widely investigated due to their high structural stability, especially iron-based polyanionic mixed phosphate Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (NFPP) has been widely studied due to its high theoretical capacity (129 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and suitable voltage plateau (~3.1 V).<span><sup><a href="#anie202423296-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423296-bib-0016_R_d29071628e558" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Chem. Eng. Sci.">5</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>However, the electrochemical performance of NFPP is limited by its lower intrinsic electronic conductivity and sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion kinetics, especially in terms of rate capacity and cyclic stability.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423296-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423296-bib-0010_R_d29071628e565" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3">3</a></sup></span> Cationic doping with heteroatoms (e.g. Mg, Al, Co, Cu and Mn) has been regarded as an effective method to regulate the lattice parameters, adjust ion transportation channel and tune the local electron density for improving the inherent electronic conductivity and Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion behavior of NFPP.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423296-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423296-bib-0017_R_d29071628e570" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6">6</a></sup></span> Moreover, the Na defects and lattice regulation induced by cationic doping leads to a reduced Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion energy barrier, which can greatly enhance the NFPP electrode reaction kinetic and reduce the lattice stress during (de)sodiation cycling.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423296-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423296-bib-0018_R_d29071628e576" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6a - Adv. Funct. Mater.">6a</a></sup></span> Meanwhile, coating the NFPP surface with carbonaceous materials (e.g., CNTs and rGO) is widely used to improve its electronic conductivity. Both cationic doping and carbonaceous materials surface coating are novel methods for modifying the structure and enhancing the Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion and electrons transportation of NFPP.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423296-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423296-bib-0021_R_d29071628e581" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7">7</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Moreover, volume changes of NFPP cathode materials during cycling can cause cracking and peeling off of the active cathode material particles, which can lead to fast electrochemical performance degradation.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423296-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423296-bib-0024_R_d29071628e586" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8">8</a></sup></span> Especially, cracks and structure collapse caused by Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion-induced stress during the (de)sodiation process. Fabricating the cathode material particles with spherical geometry can effectively alleviate the diffusion-induced stress.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423296-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423296-bib-0027_R_d29071628e591" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Mater. Sci.">9</a></sup></span> However, since the nucleation and grain growth of NFPP are usually very fast, it is highly difficult to control the morphology of polyanionic cathode materials, leading to the random aggregation and formation the irregular shaped NFPP particles by using the conventional solid-state reaction or coprecipitation method.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423296-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423296-bib-0028_R_d29071628e594" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10">10</a></sup></span> Therefore, selecting a feasible synthesis approach has become the primary challenge for the fabrication of Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> cathode materials with excellent rate capability and cyclic stability.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, a spray-drying with oxalic acid as the chelating agent combined with solid-state sintering method have been employed to fabricate the spherical trace amount of Mg/Cu co-doped Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (NFMCPP) cathode materials. The obtained spherical NFMCPP cathode materials demonstrate a high specific capacity, excellent rate capability and cyclic stability. As illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0001">1</a>, the thin carbon film even coated spherical NFMCPP surface ensures the rapid electron transport. While, the Mg/Cu co-doping can effectively mitigate the lattice volume change and promote the electronic conductivity of NFMCPP by reducing band gap between the conduction and valence bands. The effects of size and shape polydispersity on Na<sup>+</sup> ions concentration distributions and diffusion-induced stresses have been systemically investigated to understand the enhanced cyclic stability of NFMCPP.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202423296-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1b51f736-99b9-45d3-9224-ab82d0f6dec2/anie202423296-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/1b51f736-99b9-45d3-9224-ab82d0f6dec2/anie202423296-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a267f3c2-54c0-4c4d-88a2-7ba16160b651/anie202423296-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1b51f736-99b9-45d3-9224-ab82d0f6dec2/anie202423296-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202423296-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202423296" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Schematic illustration of the synthesis process of the NFMCPP sample (a). The formation mechanism of the NFMCPP (b), comparison of the NFPP and the NFMCPP samples (c), TEM image of NFMCPP and corresponding EDS elemental mapping (d).</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202423296-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202423296-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>2.96</sub>Mg<sub>0.02</sub>Cu<sub>0.02</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (NFMCPP) cathode materials have been prepared by the combination of the spray-drying and solid-state reaction method. As illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0001">1a</a>, oxalic acid has been used as the chelating agent. As oxalic acid has two carboxyl groups, which can chelate with Na<sup>+</sup>, Fe<sup>3+</sup>, Mg<sup>2+</sup>, Cu<sup>2+</sup> and HPO<sub>4</sub><sup>2−</sup> to form [Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>2.96</sub>Mg<sub>0.02</sub>Cu<sub>0.02</sub>(HPO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub>]<sub>2</sub>(C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>)<sub>5</sub>. Meanwhile, Glu with higher viscosity was used as carbon source, which has a relatively direct interaction with Fe<sup>3+</sup>, Mg<sup>2+</sup> and Cu<sup>2+</sup> ions. During spray drying, [Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>2.96</sub>Mg<sub>0.02</sub>Cu<sub>0.02</sub>(HPO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub>]<sub>2</sub>(C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>)<sub>5</sub> is inclined to partially pyrolysis and aggregate as the precursor core, while Glu with higher viscosity prefers to wrap on the precursor core surface. During high temperature sintering process, due to the difference in decomposition rates between oxalic acid and Glu, the precursor core gradually shrinks and separates from the carbonaceous shell generated from Glu with temperature increase, forming the spherical core–shell structured NFMCPP particles with tiny-void space at 550 °C (Figure <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0001">1b</a>). In NFMCPP, the Mg/Cu substitution of Fe in NFPP facilitate to reduce the band gap of NFPP, which is expected to result in an enhanced intrinsic electronic conductivity and electrode reaction kinetics (Figure <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0001">1c</a>). TEM image of spherical NFMCPP particle and corresponding elemental mappings results are shown in Figure <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0001">1d</a>, which further verifies the formation of carbon shell on the particle surface. Moreover, the tiny-void space is formed between the core and shell. For comparison, similar procedures have been used to fabricate the Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> with and without adding oxalic acid as the chelating agent, which are labeled as NFPP and NFPP-0, respectively.</p>
         
         <p>The morphology of NFPP-0, NFPP, and NFMCPP has been further studied by using SEM and TEM. NFPP-0 exhibits an irregular shape (Figure <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0002">2a</a> and Figure S3), whereas the NFPP (Figure S1 and Figure S4a) and NFMCPP (Figure <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0002">2b</a> and Figure S5) with addition of the oxalic acid chelator exhibit a regular spherical shape. The NFPP-0, NFPP and NFMCPP display a narrow particle distribution with a mean particle diameter (D50) of 11.6, 11.6 and 11.8 μm, respectively (Figure S2). Thermogravimetric (TG) analysis was employed to determine the carbon contents of these three samples (Figure S9), and the carbon content of NFPP-0, NFPP, and NFMCPP are calculated to be 12.26 wt %, 13.94 wt % and 13.7 %, respectively. Figure <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0002">2e</a> shows the Raman spectra of NFPP-0, NFPP and NFMCPP. Two characteristic carbon peaks are clearly observed, which are corresponded to the D (1350 cm<sup>−1</sup>) and G (1590 cm<sup>−1</sup>) bands, respectively. The D-band is related to the disordered and defective carbon, while the G-band is related to the graphitized carbon. The degree of graphitization of carbon-based materials can be determined by the intensity ratio of D-band and G-band (I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202423296-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423296-bib-0016_R_d29071628e788" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Chem. Eng. Sci.">5</a>, <a href="#anie202423296-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423296-bib-0032_R_d29071628e788" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chem.">11</a></sup></span> The I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> value of NFMCPP is 0.79, which is much lower than that of NFPP-0 (0.88) and NFPP (0.82). It suggests of the partial graphitization of carbon skeleton in NFMCPP, leading to an enhanced electronic conductivity.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423296-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423296-bib-0032_R_d29071628e795" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chem.">11</a></sup></span> Electrical conductivity of NFMCPP is measured to be 9.4×10<sup>−8</sup> S cm<sup>−1</sup>, which is much higher than that of NFPP for 1.3×10<sup>−8</sup> and NFPP-0 for 9.5×10<sup>−9</sup> S cm<sup>−1</sup> (Table S1). Figures <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0002">2c</a>, <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0002">d</a> and Figure S4b shows the HR-TEM image of NFPP-0, NFPP and NFMCPP. In this case, a nonuniform layer of carbon is coated on NFPP with a thickness of 3–5 nm. Inset of Figure <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0002">2c</a> reveals the lattice fringes of NFPP-0 with a spacing of 0.906 nm, corresponding to the (200) crystal plane of NFPP. While, Figure <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0002">2d</a> displays a fine carbon film with a thickness of 4 nm evenly coated on NFMCPP particle. The lattice fringes with a spacing of 0.909 nm is related to the (200) crystal plane of NFMCPP. The XRD pattern and the Rietveld refinement results for NFPP-0, NFPP and NFMCPP are shown in Figure S6, Figures <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0002">2g</a>–<a href="#anie202423296-fig-0002">i</a> and Tables S2–5, and it reveals that the cell volume decreases after Mg/Cu incorporation (Table S2). The slight shrinkage of lattice parameter of NFMCPP is mainly attributed to substitution of Fe<sup>2+</sup> ions by Mg<sup>2+</sup> and Cu<sup>2+</sup> ions with smaller ionic radius, which further verifies the successful incorporation Mg and Cu into NFPP. The content of NaFePO<sub>4</sub> in NFMCPP is calculated to be 1.61 %, and it is much lower than that of NFPP (1.99 %) and NFPP-0 (16.54 %).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202423296-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a788c09e-7eb9-4ee2-8240-1e4872e5f1d4/anie202423296-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/a788c09e-7eb9-4ee2-8240-1e4872e5f1d4/anie202423296-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/83453ebb-696d-42d0-84e4-42010717528e/anie202423296-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a788c09e-7eb9-4ee2-8240-1e4872e5f1d4/anie202423296-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202423296-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202423296" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>SEM and HRTEM images of NFPP-0 (a, c) and NFMCPP (b, d), Raman spectra (e) and Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FT-IR) (f) of NFPP-0, NFPP and NFMCPP. Rietveld refinement of the X-ray diffraction pattern of NFPP-0 (g), NFPP (h), NFMCPP (i).</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Figure <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0002">2f</a> shows the Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FT-IR) of NFPP-0, NFPP and NFMCPP. The absorption peaks observed between 400 to 770 cm<sup>−1</sup> represent O−P−O bending, and the absorption peaks located within 770 to 1020 cm<sup>−1</sup> are related to the symmetric and antisymmetric P−O−P vibrations in P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> groups. While, the absorption peaks within 1020 to 1350 cm<sup>−1</sup> are related to the P−O stretching vibrations in PO<sub>4</sub> groups.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423296-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423296-bib-0016_R_d29071628e878" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Chem. Eng. Sci.">5</a>, <a href="#anie202423296-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423296-bib-0032_R_d29071628e878" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chem.">11</a></sup></span> Figure S7 shows the crystal structure of NFPP. In addition, the surface composition and valence states of Fe, Mg, Cu and C in NFPP-0, NFPP and NFMCPP have been analyzed by using X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) (Figure S8). The Mg 1s and Cu 2p peaks are observed in Mg 1s and Cu 2p XPS spectra of NFMCPP but absent in those of NFPP-0 and NFPP (Figures S8c and d). These results further verify that Mg and Cu are successfully incorporated into NFPP.</p>
         
         <p>Figure <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0003">3a</a> and Figure S10 shows the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of NFPP-0, NFPP and NFMCPP at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> within 1.5–3.8 V. Two pairs of oxidation/reduction peaks are observed at 2.998/2.707 V and 3.268/3.175 V, which are related to the reversible Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> redox reactions. While, a small anodic peak located at 2.830 V is detected, but it is not strictly symmetrical in pair, and it matches to the cathodic peak located at 2.707 V. Moreover, multiple weak redox peaks are observed in the potential window of 3.0–3.2 V, corresponding to the different processes of Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction at various sites. Specially, one pair extra oxidation/reduction peaks located at 3.152/3.078 V for Cu<sup>2+</sup>/Cu<sup>+</sup> redox couple is detected in Figure <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0003">3a</a>. This phenomenon is attributed to that the coordination number of Na sites are not fixed, Na1 (6-coordination), Na2 (7-coordination), Na3 (5-coordination), and Na4 (6-coordination) sites.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423296-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423296-bib-0017_R_d29071628e904" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6">6</a>, <a href="#anie202423296-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423296-bib-0033_R_d29071628e904" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - ACS Appl. Mater.">12</a></sup></span> Slight changes in the crystal field would greatly affect the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/deintercalation potential of different Na sites, which is eventually reflected as additional redox peaks in the CV curves.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423296-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423296-bib-0034_R_d29071628e909" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">13</a></sup></span> With incorporation of Mg/Cu, intensity of the oxidation/reduction peaks at Na1, Na3 and N4 sites are significantly enhanced, demonstrating that Mg/Cu incorporation can effectively enhance the Na<sup>+</sup> reaction kinetics. Overall, both cyclic voltammetry (CV) and galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) tests manifest that the reaction kinetics of NFMCPP electrodes are enhanced (Figure S11, 12 and Table S7).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202423296-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/181120a3-f000-4b8b-a284-6ea5fbbddb72/anie202423296-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/181120a3-f000-4b8b-a284-6ea5fbbddb72/anie202423296-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/049e9315-95b6-4adb-b5f1-0dc1eb39c5f8/anie202423296-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/181120a3-f000-4b8b-a284-6ea5fbbddb72/anie202423296-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202423296-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202423296" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of NFMCPP at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> (a). The initial charge/discharge profiles (b) and rate performance (c) of NFPP-0, NFPP and NFMCPP. Cycling performance of NFPP-0, NFPP and NFMCPP at 1.0 C (d) and corresponding middle-point voltage of NFPP-0, NFPP and NFMCPP (e). Cycling performance of NFPP and NFMCPP at 10.0 C (f). Comparison of rate and cycling performance of NFMCPP fabricated in this work and other reported works (g).</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Figure <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0003">3b</a> shows the initial galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD) curves of NFPP-0, NFPP and NFMCPP cathodes at 0.1 C. It can be seen that NFPP-0, NFPP and NFMCPP can deliver the specific discharge capacity of 94.8, 107.4 and 112.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, with Coulomb efficiencies of 94.33, 96.84 and 97.40 %, respectively. Compared to NFPP-0 and NFPP, NFMCPP exhibits a greatly enhanced rate performance (Figures <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0003">3c</a>). NFMCPP delivers the reversible discharge capacities of 112.3, 102.5, 100.1, 97.8, 94.7, 91.8 and 89.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, 5.0, 10.0 and 15.0 C, respectively. While, NFPP-0 can only deliver the discharge capacities of 94.8, 87.6, 82.8, 74.1, 50.1, 4.1 and 0.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, 5.0, 10.0 and 15.0 C, respectively. Moreover, the discharge capacity of NFMCPP returns back to 110.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C. But the discharge capacity of NFPP-0 and NFPP can only recovers back to 93.0 and 104.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C. The discharge capacities of NFMCPP and NFPP are 100.6 and 96.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1 C with capacity retention of 99.3 % and 89.8 %, respectively, while the capacity of NFPP-0 decays significantly to 69.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with capacity retention of 89.2 % (Figure <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0003">3d</a>). The midpoint voltage decay rate for NFPP (0.9019 V) is much higher than the NFMCPP (0.0952 V), as indicated in Figure <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0003">3e</a>. In addition, NFPP showed larger impedance than NFMCPP (Figure S19 and Table S8). The lower charge transfer resistance and higher Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion coefficient of NFMCPP, thus improving the NFMCPP rate performance. NFMCPP exhibits an excellent cyclic stability at 10.0 C, it delivers a discharge capacity of 90.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles with high capacity retention of 99.13 % (Figure <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0003">3f</a>). In contrast, NFPP only exhibits a discharge capacity of 65.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10.0 C after 800 cycles with a capacity retention of only 83.01 %. The excellent rate and cyclic stability of NFMCPP is much higher than most of reported works, as summaries in Figure <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0003">3g</a> and Table S6.</p>
         
         <p>The Na<sup>+</sup> extraction order (Na3→Na1→Na4) in NFPP crystal structure during the charging process is illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0004">4a</a>, which occurs reversibly during the discharging process.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423296-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423296-bib-0019_R_d29071628e991" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6b - Sci. Bull.">6b</a></sup></span> In situ XRD was performed to study the structural evolution in the initial cycle. Figures <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0004">4e</a>–<a href="#anie202423296-fig-0004">g</a> and Figure S13b show the GCD curves and selected in situ XRD contour maps of NFMCPP within a 2θ range of 10–40°. During the charging process, the (011), (210), (022), (602) and (421) diffraction peaks shift to a larger 2θ angle, indicating that the shrinkage of NFMCPP lattice during Na<sup>+</sup> extraction process.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423296-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423296-bib-0019_R_d29071628e1003" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6b - Sci. Bull.">6b</a></sup></span> When it is charged to 3.2 V, the (222) peak gradually splits into two peaks. Conversely, these characteristic peaks return back to their initial positions after the discharging process. Besides, the (602) peak disappears when it is charged to 3.1 V, and it reappears at the end of the discharging process.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423296-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423296-bib-0035_R_d29071628e1006" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14">14</a></sup></span> Similar phenomena are observed in NFPP-0 (Figures <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0004">4b</a>–<a href="#anie202423296-fig-0004">d</a>, Figure S13a), it indicates that the entire (de)sodiation process is high reversible in NFMCPP and NFPP-0. However, the 2θ variations of (011), (210) and (022) for NFPP-0 are 0.213°, 0.408° and 0.443°, respectively. In contrast, the 2θ variations of (011), (210) and (022) for NFMCPP are reduced to 0.198°, 0.329° and 0.426°, respectively. In addition, the evolutions of lattice parameters (<i>a</i>, <i>b</i>, <i>c</i>) and volume (V) are investigated according to the in situ XRD measurements (Figures <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0004">4h</a>, <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0004">i</a> and Figure S14). As indicated in the Figures, the lattice volume change of NFMCPP during the desodiation process is 3.17 %, which is much lower than that of NFPP-0 for 6.08 %, indicating a less volume contraction and significant enhanced structural stability.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202423296-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4c037e44-3533-493c-acbc-2a645b9fcc7b/anie202423296-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/4c037e44-3533-493c-acbc-2a645b9fcc7b/anie202423296-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3c3d8ef3-fdf2-4047-ba9b-ca45f2f68fe6/anie202423296-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4c037e44-3533-493c-acbc-2a645b9fcc7b/anie202423296-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202423296-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202423296" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Schematic illustration the Na<sup>+</sup> extraction reaction mechanism in NFPP crystal structure during the charging process (a). The charge/discharge profiles (b, e), in situ XRD patterns (c, d, f, g) and lattice volume changes (h, i) of NFPP-0 (b, c, d, h) and NFMCPP (e, f, g, i) during the initial charge/discharge cycle at 0.1 C.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The density functional theory (DFT) calculations were carried out to probe the intrinsic vibrations of lattice framework before and after Mg<sup>2+</sup> and Cu<sup>2+</sup> incorporation. Due to the similar ionic radius of Mg<sup>2+</sup> (0.72 Å) and Cu<sup>2+</sup> (0.73 Å) with that of Fe<sup>2+</sup> (0.78 Å), the doped Mg<sup>2+</sup> and Cu<sup>2+</sup> are likely to replace Fe<sup>2+</sup> rather than Na<sup>+</sup> (1.02 Å). In addition, Mg and Cu prefer to occupy the Fe2 sites in NFMCPP, while Fe occupancies with relatively higher formation energy can be verified as Fe1 and Fe3 (Figures S15 and S16).<span><sup><a href="#anie202423296-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423296-bib-0019_R_d29071628e1075" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6b - Sci. Bull.">6b</a></sup></span> The different charge density (DCD) and the density of state (DOS) of NFPP and NFMCPP are analyzed to clarify the superiority of Mg/Cu co-doped NFPP for SIBs (Figure S17, Figures <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0005">5a</a> and <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0005">b</a>). The band gap between the conduction and valence bands of NFMCPP (3.6966 eV) is significantly smaller than that of NFPP (0.115 eV), which indicates that appropriate Mg/Cu co-doping can effectively promote the electronic conductivity of NFPP. Atomic force microscopy (AFM) testing results indicate that the surface of NFMCPP cathode is smoother than that of NFPP-0 (Figures <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0005">5c</a>, <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0005">d</a>). While, NFMCPP displays a high Young's modulus distribution with an average value of 50.10 GPa, and it is much higher than that of NFPP-0 for 45.57 GPa (Figure S18), indicating an enhanced mechanical property of the spherical NFMCPP cathode materials.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202423296-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/36f58277-750e-4bd2-9790-469639ef8aaf/anie202423296-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/36f58277-750e-4bd2-9790-469639ef8aaf/anie202423296-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/347e52cf-a31b-4062-84fa-75ab096395a2/anie202423296-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/36f58277-750e-4bd2-9790-469639ef8aaf/anie202423296-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202423296-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202423296" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>The density of state (DOS) of NFPP (a) and NFMCPP (b), AFM images of NFPP-0 (c) and NFMCPP (d). Concentration distribution contours of Na<sup>+</sup> ions (e, f) and Von Mises Stress contour (g, h) of differently sized spherical and elliptical particles when state of charge equals to 100 %.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Moreover, the COMSOL software has been used to simulate the Na<sup>+</sup> ions concentration distribution in NFMCPP with different morphologies. Based on the assumption of isotropic Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion, the distribution of intercalated Na<sup>+</sup> ions is symmetric in regard to the centers of the spherical or elliptical NFMCPP particles, as indicated in Figures <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0005">5e</a> and <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0005">f</a>. While, the Na<sup>+</sup> ions concentration distributions in 5 different sized particles have been investigated to understand the impact of particle size. The small particles (d<sub>1</sub>=5 μm and 2a<sub>1</sub>=7 μm) demonstrate a red color in the contour diagram, indicating the higher concentration of Na<sup>+</sup> ions, while the larger particles (d<sub>5</sub>=25 μm and 2a<sub>5</sub>=35 μm) demonstrate a blue color, indicating a lower concentration of Na<sup>+</sup> ions. The different sized particles exhibit different degrees of Na<sup>+</sup> ions intercalation, indicating that they have different microscopic average state of charges (SOCs). The concentration of Na<sup>+</sup> ions distributed dimensionless along the radial direction of small spherical particle ranges from 0.95–1, while that in small elliptical particle ranges from 0.9–0.95 along the direction of <i>a</i>-axis, demonstrating that the almost complete SOC has been achieved in the small spherical and elliptical particles. While, the average SOC of large spherical particle (d<sub>5</sub>=25 μm) and elliptical particle (2a<sub>5</sub>=35 μm) range from 0.8–1.0 and 0.1–0.9, respectively, demonstrating a lower SOC. It indicates that these large particles need additional time to become fully charged. These results show that small particles have higher microscopic average SOCs than large particles, and spherical particles have higher microscopic average SOCs than irregularly particles, illustrating that the high sphericity facilitates sufficient and fluent Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion.</p>
         
         <p>Meanwhile, the single-cell transient Von Mises Stress for differently sized spherical and elliptical NFMCPP particles at 100 % degree of sodiation have been studied and the results are shown in Figures <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0005">5g</a> and f. As indicated in the figures, the small spherical particle (d<sub>1</sub>=5 μm) and elliptical particle (2a<sub>1</sub>=7 μm) demonstrate a blue color in the contour diagram, indicating a low Von Mises Stress concentration. While, the large spherical particle (d<sub>5</sub>=25 μm) and elliptical particle (2a<sub>5</sub>=35 μm) demonstrate a red color in the contour diagram, indicating a high Von Mises Stress concentration. During the (de)sodiation cycling, the high-stress concentration on the surface region of larger spherical and elliptical particles will cause strain mismatch within the particles. Especially for the elliptical particle, the unevenly dispersed stress/strain within the particles is inclined to propagate cracks and even leads to structural collapse, resulting in a poor cyclic stability. From a geometric perspective, the strain energy density distribution in the spherical particles is much more uniform than that in the elliptical or irregularly shaped particles during Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation. The spherical particles have highly symmetrical geometric shape that is consistent and continuous in any direction, it means that when the spherical particle is acted upon by an external force, the surface of the spherical particles bears more uniform force. However, elliptical or irregularly shaped particles have different surface area to volume ratios in different directions, showing anisotropy, so the stress distribution is uneven.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423296-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423296-bib-0029_R_d29071628e1178" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10a - Adv. Mater.">10a</a></sup></span> Overall, the high sphericity structure enables a low high-stress concentration and an uniform stress/strain distribution during extensive sodiation/desodiation cycling, which is attributed to responsible for its excellent structure and long-term cyclic stability.</p>
         
         <p>Electrolyte decomposition is one of the important factors for the degradation of NFPP cathode materials during cycling, the XPS spectra of C 1s, O 1s, and F 1s for NFPP-0 after 150 cycles, for NFPP and NFMCPP electrodes after 500 cycles at 1.0 C have been studied, and the results are shown in Figures <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0006">6a</a>–<a href="#anie202423296-fig-0006">c</a>. The C1s spectra can be deconvoluted into four peaks assigned to C−C, C−O, C=O, and O−C=O (Figure <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0006">6a</a>). The C−C bond is mainly ascribed to the additives of PVDF binders and conductive super P in the electrodes, whereas C−O, C=O and O−C=O bonds are mainly assigned to the byproducts from electrolyte decomposition during cycling. Characteristic peaks of ROCO<sub>2</sub>Na (534 eV) and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (532.5 eV) are detected in the O 1s spectra for NFPP-0, NFPP and NFMCPP (Figure <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0006">6b</a>). Intensity of C−O and C=O peaks in C1s and ROCO<sub>2</sub>Na in O 1s in NFPP-0 and NFPP are much stronger than those in NFMCPP, implying that more organic components from electrolyte decomposition are presented on NFPP-0 surface.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423296-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423296-bib-0038_R_d29071628e1205" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater.">15</a></sup></span> The F 1s spectra of NFPP-0, NFPP, and NFMCPP can be deconvoluted into three peaks centered at 688.28, 687.0 and 685.25 eV, which are related to PVDF, Na<sub>x</sub>PO<sub>y</sub>F<sub>z</sub>/Na<sub>x</sub>PF<sub>y</sub> and NaF, where Na<sub>x</sub>PO<sub>y</sub>F<sub>z</sub>/Na<sub>x</sub>PF<sub>y</sub> and NaF as the products for hydrolysis of NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolyte (Figure <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0006">6c</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202423296-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423296-bib-0002_R_d29071628e1235" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1a - Adv. Energy Mater.">1a</a></sup></span> Intensity of NaF and Na<sub>x</sub>PO<sub>y</sub>F<sub>z</sub>/Na<sub>x</sub>PF<sub>y</sub> peaks in F1s for NFPP-0 is much stronger than that in NFMCPP, which implies a thicker cathode electrolyte interphase (CEI) film formed on NFPP-0 surface caused by the increased side reactions between cathode and electrolyte induced by the continuous formation of cracks during cycling. It indicates that Mg/Cu co-doing and a thin layer of carbon evenly coated on NFMCPP surface can effectively prevent electrolyte decomposition and stabilize the electrode/electrolyte interface. Figures <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0006">6d</a>–<a href="#anie202423296-fig-0006">f</a> show the SEM images of NFPP-0, NFPP and NFMCPP cathodes before and after cycling tests. The NFPP-0 particle is cracked after 150 cycles at 1.0 C (Figure <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0006">6d</a>), confirming that the unevenly dispersed stress/strain within the particle will lead to structural collapse during the extensive (de)sodiation cycling. In contrast, the spherical morphology of NFPP and NFMCPP are well maintained after 500 cycles at 1.0 C (Figures <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0006">6e</a>, <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0006">f</a>), verifying the evenly distribution of strain energy density in the spherical particle and resulting in a high structure stability.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202423296-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/be8633fd-a18b-499c-a608-dca7aae77d6a/anie202423296-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/be8633fd-a18b-499c-a608-dca7aae77d6a/anie202423296-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/585353bd-84dc-45ca-90a0-7beba0e6534f/anie202423296-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/be8633fd-a18b-499c-a608-dca7aae77d6a/anie202423296-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202423296-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202423296" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>XPS spectra of C 1s (a), O 1s (b) and F 1s (c) and SEM images (d–f) for NFPP-0 after 150 cycles, and NFPP and NFMCPP after 500 cycles at 1.0 C within 1.5–3.8 V. Schematically illustration of structural stability of NFPP-0 (g) and NFMCPP (h) particles after long-term cyclic tests.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Figures <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0006">6g</a> and <a href="#anie202423296-fig-0006">h</a> schematically illustrate the mechanisms for the enhanced structure stability of NFMCPP. The NFPP-0 particle with irregular shape suffers from severe anisotropic tensile stress at the inner region than spherical NFMCPP particle at fully-charged-state from the beginning. During extensively cycling, the center of irregular shaped NFPP-0 particle experiences more severe anisotropic tensile stress, which leads to the formation of macrocracks inside the particle. The continuous generation of macrocracks and exposure of new interfaces accelerate parasitic side reactions between cathode materials and electrolyte, resulting in the yield of deleterious by-products (e.g. Na<sub>x</sub>PO<sub>y</sub>F<sub>z</sub> and HF) and formation a thicker CEI film. While, the lattice stress is effectively mitigated by Mg/Cu co-doping in NFMCPP with a reduced volume change of only 3.17 %. Moreover, the unique core–shell structure with interconnected carbon shell and efficient void space can effectively relieve the volume expansion of NFMCPP particle during extensive (de)sodiation cycling, which eventually leads to the enhanced structural stability.</p>
         
         <p>To evaluate the practical applications of NFMCPP, SIB pouch-type full cells were assembled by using NFMCPP cathode and a HC anode (NFMCPP||HC, Figure S20a). The GCD curve of assembled pouch-type full cell at 0.2 C is exhibited in Figure S20b, and discharge specific capacity of the full cell is calculated based on the cathode weight. It can deliver a high discharge specific capacity of 105.12 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2 C. While, the pouch-type full cell exhibits the discharge capacities of 103.26, 100.15, 88.76 and 64.18 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5, 1.0, 2.0 and 5.0 C, respectively (Figure S20c), implying an excellent rate capability. Moreover, the fabricated full cell demonstrates an outstanding cycling stability with a capacity retention of 94.31 % after 1000 cycles at 1.0 C (Figure S20d). Compared to the state-of-the-art NASICON and layered oxide cathode materials, NFMCPP||HC pouch-type full cell fabricated in this work demonstrates the great superiority in average working voltage and discharge specific capacity (Figure S20e and Table S9).</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202423296-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202423296-sec-0003-title"> Conclusions</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, spherical Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>2.96</sub>Mg<sub>0.02</sub>Cu<sub>0.02</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (NFMCPP) cathode materials have been successfully synthesized via the combination of spray-drying and solid-state reaction method. The Mg/Cu co-doping can effectively mitigate the lattice volume change and promote the electronic conductivity of NFMCPP by reducing the band gap between conduction and valence bands. While, the spherical structured NFMCPP with a carbon film even coated on its surface ensures rapid electron transport and fluent Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion. Moreover, small NFMCPP particles with spherical geometry exhibits an enhanced structural stability, due to the high sphericity structure enables a low high-stress concentration and alleviated diffusion-induced stress during the extensive (de)sodiation cycling. Eventually, the fabricated spherical NFMCPP cathode materials exhibit an excellent rate capability and cyclic stability. This study provides a viable approach for advancing polyanionic compound cathodes and accelerating its wide commercial applications.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202423296-sec-0004">

         </section>

            <div class="accordion__content" style="display: none;" role="region" aria-labelledby="references-section-1">
